            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Most notable for his age last season (20), Tejada did little right in his debut. I guess you can say he walked at an average rate (8.6%) and didn't strike out a ton (17.6%), but he showed negative power (.069 ISO), didn't show great speed either (two stolen bases, two caught stealings), and didn't impress with the glove (-11.3 UZR/150 at 2B). That might be why the reports say that he will likely be returned to the minor leagues by the new administration in New York. He'll play shortstop, and his brand of contact-heavy slap hitting might play well enough if the glove translates to his new position. And if Jose Reyes gets traded, he might even see some late-season time at short. But he doesn't own a ton of upside - even if he debuted at a tender age. (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ruben Tejada debuted young, but that’s the most impressive portion of his resume. A long shot to contribute in 2011, if he does, at least it will most likely be at shortstop.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Coming up in the minor leagues, Tejada made a lot of contact, flashed a plus glove, and stole some bases. When he joined the Major Leagues, he added above-average patience to the package. Unfortunately, he doesn't offer a ton in fantasy, where his patience and glove are less useful and his lack of power is more glaring. According to the Mets hitting coach, he should start the season in the eighth spot in the order, too, so there might not be a ton of stolen bases coming. Sorry Mets fans and fantasy players, the Jose Reyes replacement will have you pining for days of yore, even if the player is decent enough in real life. (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ruben Tejada won't manage half the speed or power of his predecessor in New York, and it's unclear if his patience would survive a move to the top of the lineup. Still, he's a warm body that might manage a decent batting average at the shorstop position, so that has some fantasy value. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Since teammate Daniel Murphy had a bottom-five combination of home runs and stolen bases, Ruben Tejada has a role model nearby. There's just no way to see him putting together more than 15 or so of the two combined. He was in the bottom five of qualified hitters in batted ball distance last season and since 2009 his seasonal high in stolen bases is nine. He got caught as many times as he was successful last year -- he won't get the green light much with that sort of success rate. There's little reason to think the power will grow beyond a few more doubles here and there. He's shown good patience in the past, so maybe he can be a deep-on-base-percentage-league asset, but otherwise, he's the definition of replacement player, even at the worst position in baseball. You'll find him on your wire when your guy gets hurt, and you'll pinch your nose and you'll drop him as soon as you can. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/enosarris">Eno Sarris</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ruben Tejada is almost an icon: he's got great real-life value to the Mets while he's young and cheap, due to his contact rate, nascent patience, and good defense, but none of those things translate well to the fantasy game. You might own him for a stretch next season, but you won't be happy about it.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Tejada had the worst season of his career at the plate in 2013 by a wide margin, but even when he hits in the .280-.290 range, as he did in 2011 and 2012, he doesn't possess enough power or speed to be fantasy-relevant. The 24-year-old's career isolated power mark is just .060, not surprising given the fact that he has two home runs in 1,359 plate appearances in the majors. Add in the fact that he only has 13 steals over that span, and you have a player who doesn't have much to offer fantasy owners. (<a href="https://twitter.com/ScottStrandberg">Scott Strandberg</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Even in deep NL-only leagues, Tejada just doesn't have any value. No power, no speed, inconsistent hit tool...nothing to see here, folks.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>In deep NL-only leagues, which use on base percentage as a category, maybe Ruben Tejada could still provide value, but the Mets will look to upgrade at the position or let Wilmer Flores do his thing at short. Tejada has little to no speed or power, so it would really take more contact, even higher line-drive rates, and a lucky batting average on balls in play to post a respectable batting average. Tejada was tendered a contract, but only to back-up the middle infield.  (<a href="https://twitter.com/RotoBanter">Dan Schwartz</a>)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Ruben Tejada had found himself in the Mets’ doghouse at the onset of 2015 after several seasons of disappointing play on the field and complaints that he wasn’t keeping in proper shape. Once the successor to Jose Reyes at shortstop for the Mets, Tejada began 2015 on the bench, but after the Wilmer Flores experiment in the six hole fizzled, Tejada was tapped to take over once again. He provided just under league average offense, with only one stolen base and two home runs — well, one home run if you don’t count a supreme Domonic Brown <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/blogs/on-ruben-tejada-and-the-nature-of-inside-the-park-homers/">adventure</a> in right field. Even so, Tejada’s playing time came in fits and starts, though he was tapped to be the team’s starting shortstop in the postseason until one <a href="http://www.northjersey.com/sports/klapisch-nasty-play-could-turn-to-ugliness-at-citi-field-1.1430058?page=all">memorable</a> slide/tackle. That incident broke Tejada’s leg, and although the Mets tendered him a contract and he’s expected to be ready by spring training, he’ll still have to compete with incoming veteran Asdrubal Cabrera as well as the returning Flores and rookie Matt Reynolds for playing time. Suffice to say, Tejada shouldn’t be on the radar of any owners outside of NL-only leagues — and that’s only if he’s healthy. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Karl_de_Vries">Karl de Vries</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ruben Tejada wouldn’t rate as a mixed league shortstop under the best of circumstances, and that was before he suffered a broken leg in the playoffs. Now, having to fight for playing time with Asdrubal Cabrera, Wilmer Flores, and possibly Matt Reynolds entering spring training, he can be ignored on draft day in all but the deepest of NL-only formats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Although he isn't currently on the 40-man roster, it would be far from shocking to see Tejada spend a good chunk of the season in the majors with the Yankees. Didi Gregorius and Starlin Castro are entrenched as the starters at short and second, but aside from Ronald Torreyes — who is primarily a third baseman — they don't really have major league-ready players who can capably handle playing middle-infield defense. If any of those three get hurt — or if the Yanks decide they're not comfortable with Torreyes being their only backup infielder — Tejada's likely the next man up. That said, Tejada has no power, no base stealing ability, and is a career .252 hitter. (Scott Strandberg)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Tejada could have value to the Yankees as a glove-first bench piece, but he has nothing to offer in fantasy.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
